# Training Programs & Methodologies for Hyrox Race Preparation

## A Comprehensive Research Analysis for AI Coaching System Development

---

## 1. CrossFit Programming Principles

### 1.1 The CrossFit Methodology: "Constantly Varied, High-Intensity, Functional Movement"

CrossFit's core programming philosophy, as articulated by founder Greg Glassman and reiterated across CrossFit.com's mainsite and Level 1 certification materials, rests on three pillars:

- **Constantly Varied**: Workouts are never predictable. Movement patterns, time domains, loading, and modalities rotate to prevent adaptation and build broad physical preparedness.
- **High Intensity**: Intensity — defined as power output (force × distance / time) — is the primary driver of results. CrossFit prioritizes relative intensity over volume.
- **Functional Movement**: Multi-joint, compound movements that mirror real-world demands — squats, deadlifts, presses, pulls, carries, and locomotion.

**Application to Hyrox**: Hyrox demands exactly these qualities. The race is a chipper of eight 1-km runs interspersed with eight functional stations (ski erg, sled push, sled pull, burpee broad jumps, rowing, farmer's carry, sandbag lunges, wall balls). CrossFit's own official article, *"Why CrossFit Loves Hyrox"* (crossfit.com, January 2026), openly calls the Hyrox race format "one heck of a chipper" and draws direct parallels to classic benchmark WODs like Murph, Clovis, Filthy Fifty, and Glen. The article notes that "CrossFit athletes developed all metabolic pathways, allowing them to show repeated bouts of high-power output… sustained intensity in high-glycolytic events… while having the aerobic capacity to endure through a race that lasts approximately an hour" (Rochet, CrossFit.com, 2026).

### 1.2 Typical CrossFit Class Structure

A standard CrossFit class (typically 60 minutes) follows this template:

| Phase | Duration | Purpose |
|-------|----------|---------|
| **Warm-Up** | 10–15 min | General movement prep, specific skill activation, progressive loading |
| **Strength / Skill** | 15–20 min | Heavy barbell work (squat, deadlift, press, Olympic lifts), gymnastic skill, or specific capacity building |
| **WOD (Workout of the Day)** | 8–25 min | The conditioning piece — the heart of the session |
| **Cool-Down / Accessory** | 5–10 min | Mobility, light accessory work, breathing drills |

According to CrossFit's *Programming Basics: Part 1* (crossfit.com), coaches are taught to design workouts by first determining duration, movement functions, loading parameters, format (single modality, couplet, triplet, chipper), and priority (task vs. time). This systematic approach is something an AI coaching system should absolutely replicate.

### 1.3 Common WOD Formats

| Format | Description | Hyrox Relevance |
|--------|-------------|-----------------|
| **AMRAP** (As Many Rounds As Possible) | Time-priority: max work in a fixed window (e.g., 20 min) | Builds pacing instincts and sustained output — critical for managing energy across 8 stations |
| **For Time** (Task Priority) | Complete prescribed work as fast as possible | Directly mirrors the Hyrox race format: finish the total work as quickly as possible |
| **EMOM** (Every Minute On the Minute) | Perform set work at the start of each minute; rest with remaining time | Teaches interval pacing and work-rest transitions — perfect for simulating run-to-station transitions |
| **Chipper** | Large volume of movements performed sequentially, no repeating | The Hyrox race IS a chipper. This is the single most transferable WOD format. |
| **Tabata** | 8 rounds of 20 sec work / 10 sec rest | Useful for anaerobic capacity but less directly applicable to Hyrox's longer time domain |

### 1.4 Classic CrossFit Workouts That Transfer Well to Hyrox Preparation

**1. "Murph"** — 1-mile run, 100 pull-ups, 200 push-ups, 300 air squats, 1-mile run (with 20-lb vest). The bookended runs with high-volume bodyweight work build exactly the kind of run-under-fatigue capacity Hyrox demands. The time domain (35–60+ min) matches Hyrox race duration.

**2. "Filthy Fifty"** — 50 reps each of 10 different exercises for time. This chipper format teaches athletes to manage effort across multiple stations, pacing through varied movement demands — the fundamental Hyrox skill.

**3. "Chad" (1000-rep chipper)** — 1,000 reps of box step-ups with a 20-lb vest. This trains sustained muscular endurance and mental fortitude through monotonous, high-volume single-movement work — directly applicable to the 100 wall balls or 100m sandbag lunges.

**4. "Glen"** — 30 clean-and-jerks, 1-mile run, 10 rope climbs, 1-mile run, 100 burpees, 1-mile run. The alternating run-and-work structure is remarkably similar to Hyrox's format.

**5. A custom EMOM 40** — e.g., 10 rounds of: Min 1: Row calories, Min 2: Wall balls, Min 3: Sled push, Min 4: Rest. This teaches sustained interval work across machines and functional movements, directly simulating Hyrox race conditions.

### 1.5 What CrossFit Programming Gets RIGHT for Hyrox

- **Work Capacity / Engine Building**: CrossFit's emphasis on sustained high-power output across varied time domains builds the aerobic and anaerobic base that underlies Hyrox performance. The sport of CrossFit literally defines fitness as "work capacity across broad time and modal domains."
- **Metabolic Conditioning**: CrossFit athletes are pre-adapted to every metabolic pathway Hyrox tests — phosphocreatine (burpee broad jumps), glycolytic (sled push/pull, rowing), and oxidative (8 km of running).
- **Mental Toughness**: Long chippers and Hero WODs build the psychological resilience needed to push through the final stations of a Hyrox race. CrossFit athletes are practiced at managing discomfort.
- **Movement Familiarity**: As CrossFit.com notes, CrossFit athletes are "no strangers to the ski erg, rower, sleds, lunges, carries, or burpee broad jumps. And we invented the wall-ball shot" (Rochet, 2026).
- **Mixed-Modal Training**: The concept of combining monostructural cardio with functional movements in a single session is a CrossFit hallmark and the very essence of Hyrox.

### 1.6 What CrossFit Programming MISSES for Hyrox

- **Running Volume**: Standard CrossFit programming includes runs of 200m–1600m within WODs, but rarely prescribes dedicated running sessions. CompTrain includes running 2–3 times per week (comptrain.com/gym-programming-principles), which is ahead of most, but even this is insufficient for the 8 km total running in Hyrox. Mat Fraser himself admitted after his first Hyrox: "The stations I was keeping up just fine. But on the running, there were a couple times I was like, 'O'Keefe, chill'" (BoxLife Magazine, 2025).
- **Specific Station Work**: CrossFit rarely programs 50m sled pushes at Hyrox weights, 100m sandbag lunges, or 200m farmer's carries at prescribed loads. The volume and specificity of these movements differ significantly from CrossFit's approach.
- **Pacing Strategy**: CrossFit often rewards all-out intensity. Hyrox demands strategic pacing across 60–90+ minutes. An athlete who redlines on the sled push may lose minutes on subsequent runs — a skill CrossFit doesn't explicitly train.
- **Aerobic Base / Zone 2 Work**: CrossFit rarely prescribes dedicated low-intensity aerobic sessions. Hyrox performance is heavily dependent on aerobic capacity, which requires deliberate Zone 2 training.
- **Periodization for a Known Event**: CrossFit's "constantly varied" philosophy means it rarely peaks athletes for a specific event. Hyrox athletes need periodized 8–16 week blocks that culminate in race-day readiness.

**AI Coach Takeaway**: Steal CrossFit's chipper and EMOM formats, its scaling methodology, its emphasis on functional movement standards, and its mental toughness development. But layer in dedicated running programming, station-specific practice, and deliberate pacing education.

---

## 2. Hyrox Official Training Programs

### 2.1 Hyrox Official Training Partnerships

Hyrox does not produce a single, proprietary training app in the way CrossFit.com provides mainsite programming. Instead, Hyrox operates through **Official Online Training Partners**, listed on hyrox.com/hyrox-online-training-partners/. As of 2025–2026, these partners include:

**LEVLS** — The primary official Hyrox training partner. LEVLS offers two main programs:
- **HYROX Race**: A structured, progressive program building toward a specific race date, incorporating all 8 station movements with benchmark workouts.
- **HYROX Daily**: Ongoing training for year-round Hyrox fitness.

LEVLS uses "tried and tested progressive methodology" with benchmark workouts at each station to track improvements. The programs include structured training blocks, performance tracking, and the ability to target individual station weaknesses. LEVLS is app-based and represents the closest thing to an "official Hyrox program."

**Endur** — Partnered with Hyrox World Champion Alexander Roncevic, offering step-by-step audio and video coaching for Open and Pro categories.

**Relentless Method** — Offers personalized Hyrox training with AI adaptation, adjusting to athlete ability, equipment, and schedule. Athletes choose between fitness-focused or race-intense plans.

**Centr** — Chris Hemsworth's platform became an official Hyrox partner offering certified race-specific programs integrated with nutrition, recovery, and mindset coaching. It's a holistic platform but more generalist than specialized (ChAIron.app analysis, 2025).

### 2.2 Hyrox Academy & Master Trainer Ecosystem

Hyrox has developed a multi-level education and certification system through the **Hyrox Academy** (documented on roxlyfe.com/hyrox-365):
- **HYROX Foundation Course** ("Get Race Ready") — Entry-level certification for coaches
- **Level 1–4 Education Courses** — Progressive qualification covering periodization, biomechanics, nutrition, business strategy, and race development
- These certifications create "HYROX Performance Coaches" who implement the Hyrox methodology in gyms worldwide

The Hyrox Academy covers all aspects of athlete and performance development and is "written by industry and science-backed experts, all delivered through an innovative LMS system" (Roxlyfe, 2024). This ecosystem is important for AI coaches to understand because it establishes standardized coaching principles for the sport.

### 2.3 HAOS Training (Hunter McIntyre's Platform)

HAOS (Hunter's Academy of Strength) is built by multiple-time OCR World Champion Hunter McIntyre, who holds the Hyrox world record and MURPH world record. Key details (hyroxtrainingplans.com):

- **Price**: $39.99/month
- **Structure**: 6 days/week, ~100 min/session, 10 hours/week total commitment
- **Four Progressive Programs**: Intro Athlete → Strength → Run → Pro
- **Cycle Length**: 8–12 weeks per program phase
- **Philosophy**: A "proven yearly periodization model that maximizes agility, allowing you to build strength and endurance so you can quickly reach peak performance for any event" (pro.haostraining.com)
- **Key Differentiator**: HAOS trains for multiple racing disciplines (Spartan, Hyrox, CrossFit, Trail Running) and emphasizes the ability to pivot between sports — a generalist-specialist hybrid approach

HAOS also offers 1-on-1 coaching through Hunter McIntyre and Parker Gregory for athletes wanting individualized programming.

**AI Coach Takeaway**: HAOS's four-level progressive structure is an excellent template for an AI system — athletes self-select their level and advance systematically. The yearly periodization model that accommodates multiple race types is particularly valuable for athletes who compete in Hyrox seasonally.

### 2.4 Roxlyfe

Roxlyfe offers a popular **12-Week Hyrox Training Plan** (roxlyfe.com/12-week-training-plan/) with extensive user testimonials. Key features:
- **Multi-level scaling**: Workouts include difficulty levels so athletes can progress within the plan
- **Weekly Hyrox simulation**: One dedicated session per week mimics race conditions — a major confidence builder
- **Comprehensive coverage**: Runs of varying speeds/lengths, interval training, strength work, and Hyrox-specific simulations
- **Accessible price point**: Designed for non-professional athletes
- **Results**: Users report improvements of 3:30–5:30+ on race times, with personal bests across multiple stations

One testimonial captures the plan's appeal: "The Rox Lyfe program was everything I was looking for in a hybrid training program — thoughtful, comprehensive, challenging, and accessible to non-professional athletes" (Roxlyfe testimonials, 2025).

### 2.5 Other Notable Hyrox-Specific Programs

- **RMR Training** (rmr.training): Offers a 12-week flagship Hyrox program with native leaderboards, long-form video guidance, and community features. Host Rich Ryan's podcast covers structured programming for Hyrox including progressive overload, aerobic work, strength, and rest management.
- **ChAIron**: AI-powered training app claiming 30–40% improved race-day pacing accuracy through real-time form feedback and adaptive difficulty (ChAIron, 2025). Represents the frontier of AI coaching for Hyrox.
- **PRVN HYROX**: Tia-Clair Toomey and Shane Orr's PRVN has launched a dedicated Hyrox track with daily programming, 8-week cycles, race-day prep programs, and scaling options for all levels. Coached by James Kelly. Priced at $45/month.
- **HWPO HYROX**: Mat Fraser's HWPO offers a Hyrox prep track (5x/week standalone or 2x/week layered with other training), included with HWPO All Access membership. Sessions average ~60 minutes.

**AI Coach Takeaway**: The most successful Hyrox programs share these DNA traits: (1) structured periodization in 8–12 week blocks, (2) weekly race simulation sessions, (3) scalable difficulty levels, (4) integration of running and station work, and (5) progressive benchmarking. An AI coach should embed all five.

---

## 3. Running Programs for Hybrid Athletes

### 3.1 The 80/20 Running Principle (Matt Fitzgerald)

Matt Fitzgerald's *80/20 Running* (2014) synthesizes decades of exercise science into one actionable framework: **80% of training volume should be at low intensity (Zone 1–2), and only 20% at moderate-to-high intensity (Zone 3–5)**. This "polarized training" model, pioneered by exercise physiologist Dr. Stephen Seiler, is supported by robust evidence:

- **Seiler & Kjerland (2006)**: World and Olympic Champion endurance athletes spend approximately 75–80% of training time at low intensity, despite racing at much higher intensities.
- **Helgerud et al. (2007)**: 4×4-minute intervals at 90–95% max HR produced **10.8% VO₂ max improvement** vs. only 3.5% for long slow distance — nearly 3× the improvement.
- **Stöggl & Sperlich (2014)**: After 9 weeks, the polarized group showed superior improvements in 10k time trial, VO₂ max, running economy, AND lactate threshold vs. threshold-only training. The threshold group actually declined in some metrics.

Source: HyroxDataLab.com, *"How to Structure Your HYROX Running Training"* (2025), which provides an excellent evidence-based summary with full citations.

### 3.2 Applying 80/20 to a Hyrox Athlete at 35–40 MPW

A Hyrox athlete running 35–40 miles per week (a serious but achievable volume for competitive racers) would distribute approximately:

- **28–32 miles in Zone 2** (easy, conversational pace — 60–70% max HR)
- **7–8 miles in Zone 4–5** (tempo and interval work — 75–95% max HR)

This volume is higher than most Hyrox programs prescribe (most recommend 3–4 runs/week totaling 15–25 miles). Athletes at this mileage are likely targeting sub-60 or sub-55 minute Hyrox times where running split optimization becomes the primary performance lever.

### 3.3 Sample Week Structure for a Hybrid Athlete (35–40 MPW)

| Day | Running Session | Hyrox / Strength | Total Running |
|-----|----------------|-------------------|---------------|
| **Monday** | Easy Run, 6 mi (Zone 2) | — | 6 mi |
| **Tuesday** | Easy Run, 5 mi (Zone 2) | PM: Hyrox Station Work (sled, row, wall balls) | 5 mi |
| **Wednesday** | Tempo Run, 7 mi (2 mi warm-up, 3 mi tempo @ 75–85% HR, 2 mi cooldown) | — | 7 mi |
| **Thursday** | Recovery Run, 4 mi (Zone 1) | PM: Strength (squat, deadlift, press) | 4 mi |
| **Friday** | Intervals, 6 mi total (2 mi warm-up, 5×4 min @ 90–95% HR w/3 min jog, 2 mi cooldown) | — | 6 mi |
| **Saturday** | Easy Run, 5 mi (Zone 2) | Hyrox Race Simulation (compromised running + stations) | 5 mi |
| **Sunday** | Long Run, 8–10 mi (Zone 2) | — | 8–10 mi |
| **Weekly Total** | | | **~39–43 mi** |

**Key principles**: Easy days are truly easy. Hard days (Wednesday tempo, Friday intervals) are genuinely hard. Station work is placed on separate days or in PM sessions to allow morning running quality. The long run builds aerobic infrastructure. The Saturday simulation teaches compromised running (running immediately after heavy station work).

### 3.4 Polarized Training Model for Hybrid Athletes

The challenge for hybrid athletes doing both running and Hyrox station work is that **station work IS high-intensity training**. A heavy sled push session or a 100-rep wall ball set imposes significant physiological stress. This means:

- Station work days should be counted in the "20% hard" bucket
- Easy running days must be deliberately slow to compensate
- Total training stress (running + lifting + stations) must be managed holistically
- An athlete doing 3 hard station sessions plus 2 hard runs is at 5 high-intensity sessions/week — almost certainly too much

The practical recommendation from Hyrox Data Lab (2025) is: **3 running sessions + 2 Hyrox strength sessions per week**, with one of the three runs being Zone 2, one tempo, and one intervals. This represents a polarized approach where the 80% easy work comes from Zone 2 running and recovery days, while the 20% hard comes from tempo runs, intervals, AND station work combined.

### 3.5 Key Running Workouts Every Hyrox Athlete Should Do

**1. Zone 2 Long Run (60–90 minutes)**
- The aerobic engine builder. Increases mitochondrial density, improves fat oxidation (preserving glycogen for stations), and builds capillary networks.
- Progression: Add 5–10 min every 2–3 weeks until reaching 90 min. Don't exceed 90 min — this isn't marathon training.
- Practice race nutrition during long runs.

**2. Tempo Run (20–40 minutes at 75–85% max HR)**
- Raises lactate threshold and teaches the body to sustain "comfortably hard" effort. This pace approximates the average Hyrox running pace between stations.
- Should feel hard but sustainable — "short sentences only" on the talk test.

**3. VO₂ Max Intervals (4–8 × 3–4 min at 90–95% max HR)**
- The research-backed "magic protocol": 4×4 minutes at 90–95% max HR with 3-min active recovery (Helgerud et al., 2007).
- Maximizes VO₂ max — the ceiling that determines performance under extreme fatigue in late-race stations.

**4. Easy Runs (30–50 minutes, Zone 1–2)**
- Recovery runs that accumulate volume without adding stress. These are the "80%" that makes the "20%" possible.
- Common mistake: running these too hard. If you can't hold a full conversation, slow down.

**5. Compromised Running (Hyrox-specific)**
- Running immediately after station work (e.g., 50m sled push → immediate 1 km run).
- Teaches pacing under peripheral and central fatigue — the unique skill Hyrox demands that no pure running program addresses.
- Fraser structures this as: "800m run → row → 800m run → rest → 800m run → ski erg → 800m run" (BoxLife Magazine, 2025).

**AI Coach Takeaway**: Steal the 80/20 distribution as the governing principle for total training load. Implement Zone 2 running as non-negotiable daily programming for aerobic base. Use the 4×4 interval protocol for VO₂ max development. Most importantly, introduce "compromised running" as a Hyrox-specific training modality that pure running programs miss entirely.

---

## 5. What an AI Coaching System Should Steal from Each Program

### From CrossFit Programming (Mainsite / CompTrain)
- **Steal**: The WOD format library (AMRAP, EMOM, For Time, Chipper) as templates for Hyrox conditioning sessions
- **Steal**: CompTrain's principle of "intentional intensity" — programming movement pairings, loading, and rep schemes that fall into a "sweet spot for sustainable power output"
- **Steal**: The scaling methodology — offering Rx'd, scaled, and beginner versions of every workout so the AI can serve athletes at any level
- **Steal**: CompTrain's 24 benchmark workouts/year framework — create Hyrox-specific benchmarks (e.g., "1km row + 1km run for time") that repeat bimonthly for progress tracking

### From PRVN
- **Steal**: The 8-week cycle structure with built-in periodization — this is the optimal block length for Hyrox preparation
- **Steal**: The concept of having both a "base" (CrossFit/GPP) track and a "Hyrox-specific" track that athletes can switch between depending on proximity to race day
- **Steal**: Multi-level scaling (Level 1 through Level 3+) so the AI can auto-assign difficulty based on assessment data

### From HWPO
- **Steal**: Fraser's percentage-based strength programming — every working set prescribed as % of 1RM removes guesswork
- **Steal**: The "forced break" interval format — 4×2-min AMRAPs with 1-min rest — which increases actual intensity vs. long unbroken AMRAPs
- **Steal**: The sequential day numbering system (Day 1, Day 100, Day 400) which ensures progressive onboarding regardless of when an athlete starts

### From HAOS (Hunter McIntyre)
- **Steal**: The four-level progressive system (Intro → Strength → Run → Pro) as a user journey template
- **Steal**: The yearly periodization model that peaks athletes for specific races while maintaining year-round readiness
- **Steal**: The dual focus on strength AND running as co-equal pillars, not running bolted onto a strength program

### From 80/20 Running (Fitzgerald)
- **Steal**: The 80/20 intensity distribution as the GOVERNING PRINCIPLE for total training load (including station work in the "hard" bucket)
- **Steal**: The three-run-per-week framework: Zone 2 long run, tempo run, VO₂ max intervals — as the minimum effective running dose
- **Steal**: Heart rate zone training as the primary pacing metric, with clear zone definitions and talk-test fallbacks

### From Nick Bare Hybrid Athlete
- **Steal**: The two-a-day structure option (AM running / PM lifting) for athletes with time availability — the AI should offer this as an advanced mode
- **Steal**: The concept of running EVERY training day (even if just 3–5 easy miles) to accumulate aerobic volume without adding separate sessions
- **Steal**: Dedicated mobility days as programmed, non-negotiable sessions — not afterthoughts

### From Persist (Marcus Filly)
- **Steal**: Tempo-controlled lifting (e.g., 3-second eccentric squats) for building muscular endurance and joint resilience — directly applicable to sandbag lunges and farmer's carry
- **Steal**: The "warm-up as training" philosophy where warm-ups include loaded movements that build toward the day's work
- **Steal**: 6-week cycle length for hypertrophy/structural balance blocks in the off-season

### From Hyrox-Specific Programs (LEVLS, Roxlyfe, RMR)
- **Steal**: Weekly race simulation sessions — the single most Hyrox-specific training modality that no CrossFit or running program offers
- **Steal**: Station-specific benchmarking (e.g., time your 50m sled push, 1000m row, 100 wall balls independently) to identify and target weaknesses
- **Steal**: The "compromised running" concept — deliberately running immediately after heavy station work to train the specific skill of pacing under fatigue
- **Steal**: Progressive running integration where running volume and intensity increase as race day approaches while pure strength volume decreases (CrossFit.com's 12-week Hyrox prep model: full CrossFit → reduced CF + increased specificity → taper)

---

## 6. Synthesis: The Ideal AI Coaching Architecture

Based on this analysis, the optimal AI coaching system for Hyrox preparation should integrate:

1. **Running Engine**: 80/20 polarized model with 3–5 runs/week, auto-adjusting volume based on race proximity and athlete level (inspired by Fitzgerald, Hyrox Data Lab)
2. **Strength Foundation**: Percentage-based functional strength programming in 6–8 week cycles (inspired by HWPO, PRVN, Persist)
3. **Station-Specific Training**: Weekly rotation through all 8 Hyrox stations with benchmark tracking (inspired by LEVLS, Roxlyfe)
4. **Race Simulation**: Weekly chipper-style sessions mimicking full or partial Hyrox race conditions (inspired by Roxlyfe, PRVN Hyrox, CrossFit chipper WODs)
5. **Conditioning Variety**: CrossFit WOD formats (EMOM, AMRAP, For Time) used as templates for metabolic conditioning sessions (inspired by CompTrain, CrossFit Mainsite)
6. **Progressive System**: Multi-level athlete pathway from beginner to elite with auto-scaling (inspired by HAOS's 4-level system, PRVN's 3-level Compete)
7. **Periodization**: Year-round programming with 8–12 week race-prep blocks that peak for specific events (inspired by HAOS yearly model, PRVN cycles)
8. **Pacing Intelligence**: Zone-based training with HR monitoring, enforced easy days, and race-pace calibration (inspired by 80/20, Hyrox Data Lab's anti-gray-zone philosophy)

No single existing program scores a 10/10. The programs that come closest — PRVN Hyrox (8/10) and LEVLS (8/10) — still lack adaptive personalization and ultra-high running volume options. The opportunity for an AI coaching system is to synthesize the best elements from all programs into a truly personalized, adaptive Hyrox preparation experience.

# Scientific Foundations for an AI Hyrox Coach: Expert Frameworks, Energy Systems, and Evidence-Based Training Protocols

## Table of Contents
1. Key Figures and Their Frameworks
2. Energy Systems for Hyrox
3. Aerobic Base Development
4. Interval Training Science
5. Concurrent Training Research
6. Recovery and Adaptation Science
7. Testing and Benchmarking

---

## 1. Key Figures and Their Frameworks

### 1.1 Chris Hinshaw — Aerobic Capacity and the Endurance Engine

Chris Hinshaw is the founder of the Aerobic Capacity programming system and has coached over 35 CrossFit Games champions. A former Ironman triathlete himself, Hinshaw transitioned into coaching functional fitness athletes after observing a critical gap: most high-intensity training programs neglected the aerobic system entirely, relying almost exclusively on glycolytic and phosphagen energy systems. Hinshaw's central thesis — which he calls "building the engine" — is that an athlete's aerobic base is the mechanical foundation that allows all other physical capacities to express themselves at higher volumes and for longer durations.

**Core Philosophy: The Aerobic Engine**

Hinshaw's philosophy rests on several key principles drawn from his coaching experience and endurance sport background (Source: *PMPC Podcast #004, Chris Hinshaw – Aerobic Capacity Founder*, 2024; *Best Hour of Their Day* episodes 561-562; *Jedburgh Podcast*):

1. **Fast-twitch fibers create fatigue; slow-twitch fibers clear it.** During high-intensity work, fast-twitch muscle fibers produce lactate and other fatigue-inducing byproducts. Slow-twitch fibers consume that lactate as fuel. If an athlete only trains high intensity, they become excellent at *producing* lactate and working under its burden, but they never develop the capacity to *clear* it. This is the bottleneck — and the aerobic engine is the solution.

2. **If you have a 50/50 blend of fast-twitch and slow-twitch fibers and only train fast-twitch, you are leaving 50% of your available capacity passive.** Slow-twitch fibers require volume and time under tension to adapt. Short, explosive training sessions do not provide the overload stimulus these fibers need. This is especially critical for Hyrox, where the race lasts 60–90+ minutes and demands sustained output across diverse movement patterns.

3. **Every movement is unique, and every intensity within that movement is unique.** Hinshaw insists that athletes train across a "variety of intensities." Speed alone does not develop the full spectrum of the aerobic system. You must train the low end (base building, lactate clearance, capillarization), the moderate range (tempo and threshold work), and the high end (VO2max intervals) to build a complete engine.

4. **Pacing is the ultimate competitive advantage.** Hinshaw was one of the earliest advocates of pacing strategy in functional fitness. He teaches that efficiently and effectively consuming available energy through controlled pacing maximizes total performance. In Hyrox, where athletes must sustain effort across 8 km of running interspersed with 8 functional stations, pacing is not optional — it is the difference between a competitive finish and a collapse.

5. **Long-term cellular adaptation beats short-term intensity spikes.** While high-intensity protocols produce quick results, Hinshaw warns that these adaptations are "non-sustainable" and "riddled with risk." The real gains come from cellular-level adaptation: increased mitochondrial density, capillarization, improved fat oxidation, and enhanced stroke volume. These take months to develop but last far longer and provide a far higher ceiling.

**Hinshaw's Interval Prescriptions**

Hinshaw structures interval workouts around three key variables: **distance (or time), intensity, and rest**. He designs workouts with very specific intent — targeting either the aerobic system (VO2max zone, typically 6–9 minutes at maximum effort), the lactate clearance system (moderate work with active recovery), or the pacing system (controlled-split intervals). (Source: *Jedburgh Podcast*; *brUTE Strength Podcast Ep.10*)

| Protocol Type | Example Workout | Work Interval | Rest Interval | Intensity | Target Adaptation |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| VO2max Intervals | 3 sets × 4 × 400m run | ~2 min per rep | 40 sec between reps, 4 min between sets | Moderate to hard pace | Aerobic capacity, oxygen utilization |
| Multi-Modal Transfer | 4 × 2 min on each of bike, row, ski (12 rounds total) | 2 min per effort | Matched to running rest periods | Sport-specific moderate pace | Cross-modal aerobic transfer, RPM matching |
| Pacing Intervals | 5 × 1 km run (even-split target) | ~4–6 min each | 2–3 min walk/jog | Controlled, aiming for identical splits | Pacing discipline, metabolic efficiency |
| Active Recovery Focus | Wall balls: 20 reps + 5 slow air squats EMOM | ~45 sec work per min | Remaining time in minute (active) | Race-weight ball, race-pace reps | Lactate clearance under continued movement |
| Aerobic Base Run | 30–60 min at conversational pace | Continuous | N/A | Zone 2 (nasal breathing capable) | Capillarization, fat oxidation, slow-twitch development |

**Hinshaw's Active Recovery Principle for Hyrox:** Rather than lying on the floor between heavy sets, Hinshaw advocates keeping the body moving at low intensity — cycling slowly, doing air squats, or walking — to encourage the slow-twitch fibers to consume the lactate produced by the fast-twitch fibers. This is directly applicable to Hyrox station-to-run transitions, where the ability to recover *while still moving* is paramount. A Hyrox-adapted version: after sled pushes, immediately transition to a slow jog for the subsequent 1 km run rather than standing still, even if the first 200m feels slow. The physiological payoff is faster lactate clearance and a lower perceived effort for the remainder of the run. (Source: YouTube, "Use Hinshaw's method to increase your reps for Hyrox," 2023)

**Hyrox Relevance:** Hinshaw's framework maps almost perfectly onto the Hyrox demand profile. A Hyrox race is an 8 km run broken into 1 km segments, interspersed with 8 functional stations. More than 60% of total race time is spent running (Brandt et al., 2025, *Frontiers in Physiology*). The ability to clear lactate during running segments after high-output stations (sled push, sled pull, wall balls) is the single most trainable limiter for most recreational Hyrox athletes.

---

### 1.2 Andy Galpin, PhD — Exercise Physiology and the 9 Adaptations Framework

Andy Galpin is a Professor of Kinesiology at California State University, Fullerton, and one of the foremost authorities on bridging exercise science and practical application. His six-part guest series on the *Huberman Lab Podcast* (January–February 2023) constituted a landmark body of content on fitness science, covering everything from assessment to programming to recovery. His frameworks provide the scientific scaffolding upon which an AI Hyrox coach should build all training recommendations.

**The 9 Exercise-Induced Adaptations**

Galpin categorizes the physiological outcomes of exercise into 9 distinct adaptations. Each is elicited by manipulating modifiable training variables: exercise choice, intensity, volume, rest intervals, and progressive overload. The 9 adaptations are (Source: *Huberman Lab Guest Series*, Episode 1: "How to Assess & Improve All Aspects of Your Fitness"):

| # | Adaptation | Definition | Key Training Variable | Hyrox Relevance |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | Skill/Technique | Moving more efficiently in specific patterns | Exercise choice, practice | Running economy, wall ball technique, sled mechanics |
| 2 | Speed | Higher velocity or rate of acceleration | Low volume, max effort, full rest | Burpee broad jump explosiveness, sled push initiation |
| 3 | Power | Speed × Force | Explosive movements, moderate load | Sled push/pull drive, wall ball power |
| 4 | Strength (Force) | Maximum force production in one effort | 3–5 reps, 3–5 sets, 3–5 min rest | Sled push/pull capacity, farmers carry load management |
| 5 | Hypertrophy | Muscle size increase | 6–30 reps, 10–20 sets/muscle/week | Functional muscle mass for load-bearing stations |
| 6 | Muscular Endurance | Reps to failure in a localized muscle group | High reps, short rest, local fatigue | Wall balls (75–100 reps), sandbag lunges |
| 7 | Anaerobic Capacity | Max work at max effort, total system fatigue | 20–120 sec all-out, full recovery | Sled push/pull, ski erg, rowing erg bursts |
| 8 | Maximal Aerobic Capacity (VO2max) | Max oxygen utilization, 8–15 min domain | Intervals at 85–95% HRmax, 3–5 min work | 1 km run splits, sustained high output across race |
| 9 | Long-Duration Endurance | Sustained sub-maximal work, 15+ min, no breaks | Continuous effort, nasal breathing pace | Overall race endurance, pacing over 60–90 min |

**Galpin's Key Principle for Hyrox Athletes:** The closer two adaptations are to each other on the list, the more compatible they are for concurrent training. Speed, power, and strength (2–4) are very complementary. Muscular endurance, anaerobic capacity, and aerobic capacity (6–8) are also compatible. The challenge arises when trying to maximize adaptations at opposite ends of the spectrum simultaneously — for example, maximal hypertrophy (5) and long-duration endurance (9). This is the concurrent training problem, which Galpin addresses directly. (Source: *Huberman Lab*, Episode 4: "Optimize Your Training Program for Fitness & Longevity")

**Galpin's Programming Rules (The "3-to-5" Rule for Strength):**
- 3–5 exercises per session
- 3–5 reps per set
- 3–5 sets per exercise
- 3–5 times per week
- 3–5 minutes rest between sets

This formula is for pure strength development. For Hyrox, Galpin would recommend a blended approach across the week — some sessions targeting strength (3–5 rule), others targeting muscular endurance (high rep, short rest), and others targeting aerobic capacity (Zone 2 or interval training).

**Galpin's "Sugarcane" Protocol for Anaerobic Capacity**

One of the most practical tools Galpin shared on *Huberman Lab* (Episode 3: "How to Build Physical Endurance & Lose Fat") is the Sugarcane Protocol — a self-regulating interval workout designed to test and build anaerobic capacity:

- **Round 1:** Choose a cardio modality (bike, rower, ski erg). Go as hard as you can for a set distance or time (e.g., 500m row). Note the time.
- **Round 2:** Rest fully (2–4 minutes). Repeat the exact same distance. Try to match your Round 1 time. Note the time.
- **Round 3:** Rest fully. Now you must *beat* your average time of Rounds 1 and 2.
- **Progression:** Add rounds over weeks (3 → 4 → 5 → 6–8 rounds).

The beauty of this protocol is self-regulation: if you go out too fast in Round 1, Round 3 becomes brutally hard. If you pace too conservatively, Round 3 is easy — but your total output suffers. It teaches pacing discipline while building anaerobic capacity — exactly the dual demand of Hyrox.

**Galpin's Endurance Training Hierarchy:**

| Endurance Type | Work Duration | Fuel Source | HR Zone | RPE | Breathing |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Muscular Endurance | Repeated reps to local failure | Glycogen, PCr | Variable | 6–8/10 | Unrestricted |
| Anaerobic Capacity | 20–120 sec max effort | Glycogen (anaerobic glycolysis) | Zone 4–5 | 9–10/10 | Mouth, forced |
| Max Aerobic Capacity (VO2max) | 4–8 min intervals | Glycogen + Oxidative | Zone 4–5 | 8–9/10 | Mouth, labored |
| Long-Duration Endurance | 15–60+ min continuous | Fat (primary), Glycogen (secondary) | Zone 2 | 4–6/10 | Nasal only (ideally) |

(Source: *Huberman Lab*, Episode 3; *Momentous/Galpin* 9 Adaptations guide)

---

## 2. Energy Systems for Hyrox

### 2.1 The Three Energy Systems: How They Interact During a 60–90 Minute Race

All three energy systems — phosphagen (ATP-PCr), glycolytic, and oxidative — operate simultaneously during any physical activity. What changes is their *relative contribution* based on intensity and duration. In Hyrox, the oxidative system is the dominant provider of ATP across the full race duration, but the glycolytic and phosphagen systems play crucial roles at specific stations and transitions.

**The Brandt et al. (2025) Study — First Peer-Reviewed Hyrox Research**

This landmark study (Brandt, Ebel, Lebahn, & Schmidt, 2025; *Frontiers in Physiology*, 16, doi: 10.3389/fphys.2025.1519240) provides the most rigorous physiological data on Hyrox to date. Key findings from 11 recreational Hyrox athletes completing a simulated competition:

| Metric | Full Hyrox | Running Segments | Exercise Stations |
|---|---|---|---|
| Completion Time (median) | 86.5 min | 51.2 min (59%) | 32.8 min (38%) |
| HR max (bpm) | 185 | 180 | 185 |
| HR avg (bpm) | 170.9 | 168.9 | 173.7 |
| Blood Lactate max (mmol/L) | 8.5 | 7.7 | 8.5 |
| RPE max (Borg 6–20) | 18 | 16 | 18 |
| Time at "Very Hard" intensity (% HRmax) | 79.5% of race | — | — |

**Key Takeaways for Coaching:**
- Hyrox is performed overwhelmingly at **hard to very hard intensity** (79.5% of total time above 80% HRmax).
- **VO2max was the strongest correlate of overall race time** (ρ = −0.71, p = 0.01), followed by endurance training volume (ρ = −0.68) and body fat percentage (ρ = 0.67).
- **Strength measures (hand grip strength, muscle mass) did NOT correlate with completion time.** Once athletes can handle the competition loads, further strength gains yield diminishing returns.
- The **exercise stations were more intense than the runs** by every metric (HR, lactate, RPE), but took less time.
- The **heaviest stations (sled push, sled pull) were completed fastest** — typically 2–3 minutes — placing them in the glycolytic/anaerobic capacity domain.
- **Wall balls produced the highest HR, lactate, and RPE** — occurring last in the race when cumulative fatigue is maximal.

### 2.2 Energy System Demands by Hyrox Station

| Station | Primary Energy System | Duration (typical) | Key Demand | Training Implication |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 1 km Run (×8) | Oxidative (primary), Glycolytic (secondary) | 4–7 min each | Sustained aerobic output, lactate clearance between stations | Zone 2 base, threshold running, fatigued-state running |
| Ski Erg (1000m) | Oxidative + Glycolytic | 3–5 min | Upper body aerobic endurance, sustained power | Aerobic intervals on ski erg, muscular endurance |
| Sled Push (4×12.5m) | Phosphagen + Glycolytic | 2–3 min | Maximal force production, rapid depletion | Strength-endurance, heavy sled practice, power endurance |
| Sled Pull (4×12.5m) | Phosphagen + Glycolytic | 2–3 min | Grip + posterior chain under heavy load | Grip endurance, rowing/pulling strength-endurance |
| Burpee Broad Jump (80m) | Glycolytic + Oxidative | 3–5 min | Repeated explosive effort, global fatigue | Plyometric endurance, pacing short explosive reps |
| Row Erg (1000m) | Oxidative + Glycolytic | 3–5 min | Full-body aerobic power, pacing | Aerobic intervals on rower, threshold pacing |
| Farmers Carry (200m) | Oxidative (low intensity per step) | 2–3 min | Grip endurance, loaded locomotion | Loaded carries, grip training, trunk stability |
| Sandbag Lunges (100m) | Glycolytic + Oxidative | 3–5 min | Muscular endurance (quads, glutes), balance | High-rep lunging, leg endurance, unilateral strength |
| Wall Balls (75–100 reps) | Glycolytic (dominant by end) | 3–6 min | Muscular endurance (quads, shoulders), lactate tolerance | High-rep wall balls, EMOM sets, active recovery between sets |

(Sources: Brandt et al., 2025; Warrior Performance Lab analysis, 2025; HYROX Training Plans programming guide)

### 2.3 How to Train Each Energy System for Hyrox

| Energy System | Training Protocol | Work:Rest Ratio | Sets × Duration | Intensity | Frequency/Week |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Phosphagen (ATP-PCr) | Heavy sled pushes, max sprints | 1:12–1:20 | 6–10 × 5–10 sec | 95–100% max effort | 1–2× (in strength sessions) |
| Glycolytic (Fast) | Sled push/pull repeats, ski erg intervals | 1:3–1:5 | 4–8 × 30–90 sec | 85–95% max effort | 1–2× |
| Glycolytic (Slow) | Wall ball EMOMs, sandbag lunge sets, threshold intervals | 1:1–1:2 | 3–6 × 2–5 min | 75–85% max effort | 2–3× |
| Oxidative (VO2max) | Norwegian 4×4, Hinshaw-style intervals | 1:0.75 | 4 × 4 min | 85–95% HRmax | 1–2× |
| Oxidative (Base/Zone 2) | Easy run, bike, row, hike | Continuous | 1 × 30–90 min | 60–70% HRmax, nasal breathing | 2–4× |

---

## 3. Aerobic Base Development

### 3.1 Zone 2 Training: What It Is, Why It Matters, How Much Is Enough

Zone 2 training refers to exercise performed at or just below the first lactate threshold (LT1), typically defined as the intensity at which blood lactate first rises above resting levels (~2 mmol/L). At this intensity, the body predominantly uses fat as fuel, the effort is sustainable for long durations, and the athlete can breathe through the nose or hold a conversation. (Source: Iñigo San-Millán, PhD; CTS/TrainRight, 2025)

**Physiological Adaptations of Zone 2 Training:**

| Adaptation | Mechanism | Why It Matters for Hyrox |
|---|---|---|
| Increased mitochondrial density | Biogenesis of mitochondria in slow-twitch fibers | More energy production capacity per muscle cell |
| Enhanced fat oxidation | Upregulation of fatty acid transport and beta-oxidation enzymes | Preserves glycogen for high-intensity stations |
| Improved lactate clearance | Increased MCT1 transporters, slow-twitch fiber lactate shuttling | Faster recovery between stations and runs |
| Increased capillarization | Growth of new capillary beds around muscle fibers | Better oxygen delivery and waste removal |
| Greater stroke volume | Heart adapts to pump more blood per beat | Lower heart rate at any given submaximal pace |
| Increased plasma volume | Expansion of blood volume | Better thermoregulation, improved cardiac output |

**Zone 2 Intensity Guidelines:**

| Method | Zone 2 Range | Practical Cue |
|---|---|---|
| % of Max Heart Rate | 60–70% HRmax | Can hold a conversation, slight breathlessness |
| % of Lactate Threshold HR | 68–83% of LT2 HR | Comfortably challenging, not hard |
| RPE (1–10) | 3–5/10 | "I could do this for a long time" |
| Blood Lactate | 1.5–2.5 mmol/L | Below first lactate threshold |
| Talk Test | Can speak in full sentences | Lose the ability = too hard |
| Breathing | Nasal breathing achievable | If you must mouth-breathe, slow down |

(Sources: San-Millán & Brooks, 2018; CTS/TrainRight "Blood Lactate in Training" article; Galpin, *Huberman Lab* Episode 3)

**How Much Zone 2 Is Enough?**

The polarized training model used by elite endurance athletes suggests approximately 80% of total training volume should be in Zone 1–2, with the remaining 20% at high intensity (Zone 4–5). For a Hyrox athlete training 6–10 hours per week:

| Weekly Training Volume | Zone 2 Target | Zone 2 Sessions | Format |
|---|---|---|---|
| 6 hours/week | 3–4 hours | 2–3 sessions of 60–90 min | Easy runs, bike rides, row, hike |
| 8 hours/week | 4–5 hours | 3–4 sessions of 60–80 min | Mixed modality (run, bike, row) |
| 10 hours/week | 6–7 hours | 4–5 sessions of 60–90 min | Primarily running + cross-training |

Galpin's recommendation for long-duration endurance: perform 15+ minutes of continuous work at a pace where you can maintain nasal breathing. For most Hyrox athletes, this is a run, bike, or row at an easy, conversational effort. Two sessions per week is the minimum effective dose; three to four is optimal for building a robust aerobic base while leaving room for high-intensity and strength work. (Source: *Huberman Lab*, Episode 3)

### 3.2 The MAF (Maximum Aerobic Function) Method

The MAF method, developed by Dr. Phil Maffetone, uses a simple formula to estimate Zone 2 heart rate: **180 − age = MAF HR ceiling**. This is then adjusted by fitness status (e.g., +5 for consistent athletes, −5 for recovering from injury/illness).

| Age | MAF HR (Base) | Adjusted (Fit Athlete) | Adjusted (Returning from Injury) |
|---|---|---|---|
| 25 | 155 bpm | 160 bpm | 150 bpm |
| 30 | 150 bpm | 155 bpm | 145 bpm |
| 35 | 145 bpm | 150 bpm | 140 bpm |
| 40 | 140 bpm | 145 bpm | 135 bpm |

**Hyrox Application:** The MAF method is useful as a *starting point* for Hyrox athletes who don't have access to lactate testing. However, it is less accurate than lactate-based zone setting, and athletes with high fitness levels may find it overly conservative. Use MAF as a floor — if your easy runs feel "too slow" at MAF heart rate, that's actually the point. Trust the process. Over 8–12 weeks, MAF pace will improve (same heart rate, faster pace), which is direct evidence of aerobic base improvement.

### 3.3 Lactate Threshold Training for Hyrox Pacing

The lactate threshold (LT2) represents the intensity above which lactate accumulates faster than the body can clear it. For Hyrox, the goal is to push LT2 as high as possible so that race pace (which is predominantly aerobic) represents a smaller fraction of maximal capacity.

**Lactate Threshold Interval Protocols:**

| Protocol | Work Interval | Rest | Sets | Intensity | Target |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Tempo Runs | 20–40 min continuous | N/A | 1 | ~85–88% HRmax, "comfortably hard" | LT2 improvement |
| Cruise Intervals | 3 × 10–15 min | 2–3 min jog | 3 | At or slightly below LT2 | Sustained threshold capacity |
| Threshold Repeats | 4 × 8 min | 2–3 min | 4 | At LT2 pace | Lactate tolerance and clearance |
| Progressive Run | 30–45 min, last 10–15 min at threshold | N/A | 1 | Build from Zone 2 → LT2 | Pacing discipline, metabolic transition |

(Sources: Kristensen et al., as cited in *PMC* zone 2/3 interval review, 2023; Helgerud et al., 2007)

**Hyrox-Specific Application:** A Hyrox race is best paced at or just below lactate threshold for the running segments. The stations will push blood lactate above threshold. The runs must therefore be paced conservatively enough to allow partial lactate clearance. Training at threshold teaches the body where that "redline" is and expands it over time. A weekly schedule might include one tempo or threshold session and one true Zone 2 session as the endurance components alongside interval and strength work.

---

## 4. Interval Training Science

### 4.1 Interval Protocol Comparison Table

| Protocol | Work Duration | Rest Duration | Intensity | Sets/Reps | Primary Adaptation | Key Study |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Norwegian 4×4** | 4 min | 3 min active recovery | 85–95% HRmax | 4 intervals | VO2max (+11–13% in 8 weeks) | Helgerud et al., 2007 |
| **Tabata** | 20 sec | 10 sec | All-out (170% VO2max) | 8 rounds (4 min total) | Anaerobic capacity, VO2max | Tabata et al., 1996 |
| **30/30 Intervals** | 30 sec | 30 sec active | 90–100% vVO2max | 12–20 rounds | Time at VO2max, buffering | Billat et al., 2001 |
| **Long Intervals** | 3–5 min | 2–4 min | 90–95% HRmax | 4–6 sets | VO2max, cardiac output | Seiler & Sjursen, 2004 |
| **Short Intervals** | 10–30 sec | 20–60 sec | Maximal sprint | 6–10 sets | Phosphagen repletion, speed | Ross & Leveritt, 2001 |
| **Threshold Repeats** | 8–15 min | 2–3 min | LT2 pace | 3–4 sets | Lactate threshold elevation | — |
| **Hinshaw Multi-Modal** | 2 min per modality | 40 sec between reps, 4 min between sets | Moderate–hard | 3 sets × 4 reps | Cross-modal aerobic transfer | Hinshaw (practitioner) |
| **Galpin Sugarcane** | Variable (set distance) | 2–4 min full recovery | Self-regulated (escalating) | 3–8 rounds | Anaerobic capacity, pacing | Galpin (practitioner) |

### 4.2 The Norwegian 4×4 Protocol — Gold Standard for VO2max

The Norwegian 4×4 is arguably the most well-validated interval protocol for improving VO2max. Developed by researchers at the Norwegian University of Science and Technology (NTNU), the protocol is:

- **Warm-up:** 10 minutes at easy pace
- **Work:** 4 × 4 minutes at 85–95% of HRmax
- **Recovery:** 3 minutes of active recovery (walking or easy jogging) between intervals
- **Cool-down:** 5–10 minutes easy

**Evidence:** Helgerud et al. (2007, *Medicine & Science in Sports & Exercise*) demonstrated a 13% increase in VO2max in just 8 weeks with this protocol, compared to only 3.2% with steady-state training of equivalent total work. Wisløff et al. (2007) showed that 4×4 intervals improved cardiac function and reversed heart damage in heart failure patients. A study of untrained 50-year-olds showed that performing the Norwegian 4×4 just once per week for two years made their hearts functionally 20 years younger. (Sources: Science for Sport, 2025; Fysiofitaal analysis; Helgerud et al., 2007)

**Why This Matters for Hyrox:** VO2max was the strongest single predictor of Hyrox race performance (Brandt et al., 2025). The 4-minute work intervals fall right in the 6–9 minute "VO2max time domain" that Hinshaw identifies as the sweet spot for improving the ability to bring in and utilize oxygen. One to two sessions per week of Norwegian 4×4 intervals on the rower, bike, ski erg, or running track should be a staple for any Hyrox athlete in their general preparation phase.

### 4.3 How to Periodize Intervals Leading into a Hyrox Race

| Weeks Out from Race | Phase | Interval Focus | Example Session | Frequency |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 16–12 | Base Building | Zone 2 + short introduction to intervals | 1 × Norwegian 4×4 per week + 2–3 Zone 2 sessions | 1 interval + 2–3 Zone 2 |
| 12–8 | Aerobic Development | VO2max intervals + threshold work | Norwegian 4×4 (1×/wk) + threshold repeats (1×/wk) + Zone 2 (2×/wk) | 2 intervals + 2 Zone 2 |
| 8–4 | Race-Specific | Hyrox simulation intervals, station-to-run transitions | Race-pace station work + 1 km runs (1×/wk) + 30/30 intervals (1×/wk) + Zone 2 (1–2×/wk) | 2 intervals + 1–2 Zone 2 |
| 4–2 | Sharpening | Reduce volume, maintain intensity | Short sharp intervals (Tabata or 30/30) 1×/wk + 1 easy run + 1 race-pace session | 1–2 sessions, reduced volume |
| 2–0 | Taper | Reduce volume 40–60%, maintain intensity | 1 short interval session + 1 easy run + rest | Minimal, quality over quantity |

---

## 5. Concurrent Training Research

### 5.1 The Interference Effect: What the Science Actually Says

The "interference effect" was first documented by Hickson (1980), who showed that untrained men doing both strength and endurance training simultaneously had reduced strength gains compared to those doing strength training alone. This spawned decades of concern that "cardio kills gains."

**The Molecular Mechanism (Simplified):**

- **Resistance training** activates the **mTOR pathway** (Mammalian Target of Rapamycin), which drives muscle protein synthesis — the process of building new muscle tissue.
- **Endurance training** activates the **AMPK pathway** (AMP-activated protein kinase), which restores cellular energy balance by upregulating catabolic processes and inhibiting anabolic processes, including mTOR.
- These two pathways were once thought to act as a molecular "switch" — you're either building muscle (mTOR) or generating energy (AMPK), but not both simultaneously.

**What Modern Research Shows:**

Three major meta-analyses have now concluded that **concurrent training does not significantly compromise strength or hypertrophy gains** compared to resistance-training-only programs, provided total training load is managed appropri

     - Estimated duration
     - Key focus/intent of each session
   - Must preserve 35-40 mpw running base

   - Each workout should target specific Hyrox stations or energy systems

   - How to progress station-specific work (heavier sleds, more reps, etc.)

   - What to cut, what to maintain

   - When to test what (time trials, station benchmarks, simulations)
   - Key metrics to track weekly
   - Decision points: "if X, then adjust Y"

IMPORTANT OUTPUT REQUIREMENTS:
- Length: This must be the LONGEST document — at minimum 5,000-8,000 words. This is the core programming engine for the AI coach. Do not summarize anything.

- Every workout must be fully prescribed: movements, sets x reps @ intensity (RPE or %1RM), rest periods, time caps.
- HIIT session library: Each of the 15-20 workouts needs complete prescriptions, not just descriptions.
- Decision trees for autoregulation: "If athlete reports RPE > 8 on Tuesday intervals, then reduce Thursday volume by X%"
- Be extremely specific and actionable — an AI coach will use this to generate real training plans for real athletes.
